Obligation to Accept Judicial Office When Specifically Required
Explaining the obligation of a qualified qadi when specifically required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the obligation of a qualified qadi to accept judicial office when he is the only qualified candidate, and apply the condition of ta'ayyun to scenarios of appointment.

Core Idea
A qualified qadi must accept the post when no other qualified person exists; otherwise he sins.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the communal responsibility of qada. When a qualified jurist becomes the only suitable candidate for the judiciary, accepting the position becomes wajib. If he refuses, he incurs sin. The text also addresses the situation where an ignorant person is available, clarifying that this does not relieve the qualified person of his obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on accepting qada
Understand the act: a qualified jurist (alim) who becomes specifically required (muta'ayyin) for the judiciary must accept. The ruling is wajib; refusal is sinful.
This is contrasted with the case where he is not muta'ayyin, where leaving is preferable.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is the statement of the early scholars: 'العالم إذا تعين للقضاء وجب عليه قبوله'. This is a qawl sahabi, which is a source of law in the Hanafi school.
The statement explicitly links obligation to the condition of being specifically required.
3
Analyze the condition of the ignorant judge
The text raises: when does an ignorant person become eligible? The author clarifies that the existence of an ignorant person does not prevent the qualified person from being specifically required. If there is no other qualified, the qualified must accept even if an ignorant is available.
This shows the priority of qualified judges. The ignorant's appointment is valid only if the qualified refuses and there is no other.
4
Determine the consequence of refusal
If the qualified person refuses when he is muta'ayyin, he sins (أثم). This underscores the obligatory nature.
The sin is a legal consequence, indicating the ruling is wajib, not just mandub.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحسن بعض الحوادث والمسائل الدقيقة وأن يعرف طريق تحصيل الأحكام الشرعية من كتب المذهب وصدور المشايخ وكيفية الإيراد والإصدار في الوقائع مع الدعاوى والحجج ويدل على ذلك قولهم : العالم إذا تعين للقضاء وجب عليه قبوله وإذا تركه أثم وما لم يتعين فالترك أفضل، وإذا كان الجاهل أهلاً للقضاء فمتى يتعين. قال في النهر وأقول : وجود الجاهل لا يمنع من تعينه وذلك أنه إذا لم يوجد غيره ولم يقبل أثم وإن وجد جاهل تصح توليته
He is skilled in some incidents and subtle issues, and he knows the way of deriving legal rulings from the books of the madhhab and the statements of the shuyukh, and the manner of presenting and issuing in events with claims and proofs. That is indicated by their statement: The jurist (alim) when he becomes specifically required (muta'ayyin) for the judiciary, acceptance becomes obligatory upon him, and if he leaves it, he sins. And as long as he is not specifically required, leaving is better. And if the ignorant person is eligible for the judiciary, then when does he become specifically required? He said in al-Nahr: I say: The existence of an ignorant person does not prevent him from being specifically required. That is because if there is no one else and he does not accept, he sins; and if an ignorant person exists, his appointment is valid.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage discusses the obligation of a qualified qadi to accept the judicial position and the status of an ignorant judge. It states that a jurist (alim) who is qualified and becomes specifically required (muta'ayyin) for the post of qadi must accept it, and if he refuses, he sins. If he is not specifically required, it is better to decline. It raises the question: when does an ignorant person become eligible for qada? The author of al-Nahr notes that the presence of an ignorant person does not prevent the qualified person from being specifically required. If there is no other qualified person and the qualified person refuses, he sins. But if there is an ignorant person available and the qualified person refuses, the appointment of the ignorant person is valid. This highlights the communal obligation of qada and the responsibility of qualified jurists to serve.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun): When a qualified person becomes the only one available and required for a position.
جاهل (jahil): One who does not fulfill the conditions of a qadi, i.e., lacks sufficient knowledge.
القضاء (al-qada): The judicial office in Islamic law, responsible for adjudicating disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a qualified qadi is specifically required (muta'ayyin) and refuses to accept?
A) He is excused
B) He commits a sin
C) He is forced to accept
D) He is praised

Key Takeaway
When a qualified qadi is the only qualified candidate, accepting the post is wajib; refusal incurs sin, and the presence of an ignorant candidate does not remove this obligation.

Ruling on Transferring from One Madhhab to Another
Intra-Hanafi dispute on transfer of madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi views on transferring between madhhabs and apply the principle that real taqlid occurs only upon acting on a specific ruling.

Core Idea
You can change madhhabs for new issues, but once you act, that ruling is binding for that case.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common question: can a layperson switch madhhabs? Some argue that once you commit to a madhhab you are stuck, but Ibn Nujaym shows that the Quranic command 'ask the people of remembrance' only applies when a specific issue arises. Real taqlid occurs only in acting on a specific ruling, not in a general pledge. This lesson will help you navigate the key intra-Hanafi debate on transfer and understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on transfer
Identify the range of opinions within the Hanafi school regarding whether a layperson may transfer from one madhhab to another. Note the distinction between a general commitment and a specific acted-upon ruling.
The passage primarily presents the view that transfer is permissible for new issues.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Some Hanafis (like al-Khatib) and scholars from other schools argue that transferring requires ijtihad or taharri, implying that a layperson cannot simply switch. Their proof is the need for consistency and fear of following rukhas.
The opposing view is concerned about talfiq and seeking concessions.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opponent
The author uses the verse 'Ask the people of remembrance' (16:43) to argue that the obligation to ask a mujtahid arises only when a specific incident occurs. There is no legal evidence for permanent commitment to one mujtahid.
The verse does not oblige a lifelong allegiance.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: if someone acts on a ruling from one madhhab, they cannot later adopt a contradictory ruling for the same issue. However, for new issues, they may follow any mujtahid they choose.
This prevents cherry-picking in the same case but allows flexibility across different cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا: المنتقل مذهب إلى مذهب باجتهاد وبرهان آلم يستوجب التعزير فبلا اجتهاد وبرهان أولى، ولا بد يراد بهذا الاجتهاد معنى التحري وتحكيم القلب لأن العامي ليس له اجتهاد. ثم حقيقة الانتقال إنما تتحقق في حكم مسألة خاصة قلد فيه وعمل به وإلا فقوله قلدت أبا حنيفة فيما أفتى به من المسائل والتزمت العمل به على الإجمال وهو لا يعرف صورها ليس حقيقة التقليد، بل هذا حقيقة تعليق التقليد أو وعد به كأنه التزم أن يعمل بقول أبي حنيفة فيما يقع من المسائل التي تتعين في الوقائع فإن أرادوا هذا الالتزام فلا دليل على وجوب اتباع المجتهد المعين بالتزام نفسه ذلك قولاً أو نية شرعاً بل الدليل اقتضى العمل بقول المجتهد فيما احتاج إليه بقوله تعالى فاسألوا أهل الذكر أن كنتم لا تعلمون) [النحل: ٤٣] والسؤال إنما يتحقق عند طلب حكم الحادثة المعينة، وحينئذ إذا ثبت عنده قول المجتهد وجب عمله به . والغالب أن مثل هذا إلزامات منهم لكف الناس عن تتبع الرخص وإلا أخذ العامي في كل
And they said: One who transfers from a madhhab to a madhhab with ijtihad and proof and then regrets, deserves ta'zir. Without ijtihad and proof is more deserving (of blame). There is no avoiding that by this ijtihad is meant the meaning of taharri (searching) and arbitrating the heart, because the layman has no ijtihad. Then the reality of transfer only occurs in the ruling of a specific issue in which he imitated and acted. Otherwise, his statement, 'I have imitated Abu Hanifa in the issues he gave fatwa on, and I committed to act upon it in general,' while he does not know their forms, is not the reality of taqlid. Rather, this is the reality of suspending taqlid or a promise, as if he committed to act according to Abu Hanifa's saying in the issues that occur in events. If they mean this commitment, there is no evidence for the obligation of following a specific mujtahid by his commitment to himself, verbally or by intention, legally. Rather, the evidence necessitates acting upon the saying of the mujtahid in what one needs, by His saying, the Exalted: 'So ask the people of remembrance if you do not know' [an-Nahl 16:43]. And asking only occurs when seeking the ruling of a specific incident, and at that time, when the saying of the mujtahid is established with him, acting upon it becomes obligatory. And predominant is that such restrictions are from them to prevent people from seeking rukhas (concessions); otherwise, the layman would take in every...
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of a person transferring from one madhhab to another. It states that if one transfers with ijtihad and proof, but then regrets, such a person deserves ta'zir (disciplinary punishment). If he transfers without ijtihad and proof, it is even more blameworthy. However, the ijtihad intended here is actually taharri (inner search) and following one's heart, because a layperson has no true ijtihad. Then it clarifies the reality of transfer: it only occurs in a specific ruling that one has already acted upon in taqlid. If a person says 'I have followed Abu Hanifa in all rulings and commit to act on them in general' without knowing the specifics, that is not real taqlid but a suspension of taqlid or a promise. There is no evidence that one must commit to a specific mujtahid by oath or intention. The Quranic verse 'Ask the people of remembrance' (16:43) indicates that one asks when a specific issue arises. Therefore, the ruling is that when a specific issue presents itself and a person learns the word of a mujtahid, he must act on it. The author then notes that those who forbid transfer are likely only trying to prevent people from seeking rukhas (concessions), otherwise a layperson would always take the easiest opinion.

Key Terms
انتقال (intiqal): Changing from one madhhab to another in a specific ruling or in general.
تقليد (taqlid): Following a mujtahid without knowing his evidence; here, real taqlid occurs only when one acts on a specific ruling.
تتبع الرخص (tatabbu' al-rukhas): Seeking the easiest rulings from different madhhabs, which is discouraged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does real taqlid (صحيح التقليد) occur?
A) When one declares commitment to a madhhab
B) When one memorizes the sayings of a mujtahid
C) When one acts on a specific ruling of a mujtahid
D) When one learns the proofs of a madhhab

Key Takeaway
A layperson may change madhhabs for new issues, but once a ruling is acted upon, it becomes binding for that specific case.

Qada — Judiciary
advanced
10 min
The Layperson's Obligation of Taqlid and Choosing Between Mujtahids
Rulings on taqlid, transfer between madhhabs, and the reality of following a mujtahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the layperson is obligated to follow a qualified mujtahid, explain the preferential option of following one's heart's inclination when mujtahids differ, and distinguish between actual taqlid and a mere commitment to a madhhab.

Core Idea
The layperson must follow any qualified mujtahid; he may choose based on his heart's inclination, and a general commitment to a madhhab is not binding taqlid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a fundamental question every layperson faces: when I need a fatwa, whom do I follow? The author clarifies that the obligation is not to follow a specific imam forever, but to act on the ruling of any qualified mujtahid for each specific incident. It also tackles the nature of taqlid and the controversial issue of transferring between madhhabs after having acted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the layperson's obligation
The text explicitly states 'الواجب عليه تقليد مجتهد' – the obligation upon the layperson is to follow a mujtahid. This is a wajib level derived from the command to ask the people of remembrance.
The obligation attaches to the act of seeking a ruling, not to following a particular imam.
2
Understand the choice between conflicting mujtahids
When two mujtahids give conflicting fatwas, the preferable (الأولى) course is for the layperson to follow the one his heart inclines to. However, it is permissible to take the other opinion because the obligation is satisfied by following any mujtahid.
The text says 'عندي أنه لو أخذ بقول الذي لا يميل إليه قلبه جاز' – taking the less inclined opinion is still valid.
3
Examine the Quranic proof (dalil)
The verse 'فاسألوا أهل الذكر إن كنتم لا تعلمون' (Quran 16:43) is cited. The imperative 'fas'alu' indicates obligation (wujub) according to Hanafi usul, and 'ahl al-dhikr' is interpreted as qualified scholars/mujtahids.
The proof is Quranic, with qati' thubut but zanni dalala as to who exactly 'ahl al-dhikr' are.
4
Differentiate between real taqlid and mere commitment
The author explains that true taqlid only occurs when one acts on a specific ruling. Simply saying 'I follow Abu Hanifa' without knowing the specific issues is not real taqlid but a promise. The obligation arises when a specific incident occurs.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the permissibility of changing madhhabs.
5
Analyze the dispute on retracting taqlid after acting
The text reports a claimed consensus that one cannot retract a taqlid after acting on it. However, later Hanafi authorities (Ibn al-Humam, al-Sharnbulali) nuance this: retraction is prohibited only in the same specific act (ayn), but one may follow a different mujtahid in a new act (jins).
The mufta bihi position allows following a different mujtahid in a new matter.

اجتهاده اجتهادهم فلا بأس وإن خالفه أحياناً، ومن لم يبلغ رتبة الاجتهاد بل وقف على أصول إمامه
Book of Judiciary: From that and its errors. Based on this, if a layperson asks two jurists, meaning mujtahids, and they differ with him, the preferable course is that he takes what his heart inclines to from them. In my view, if he takes the statement of the one to whom his heart does not incline, it is permissible, because that inclination and its absence are equal. The obligation upon him is to follow a mujtahid, and he has done so; that mujtahid is either correct or mistaken. They said: The one who transfers from a madhhab to a madhhab with ijtihad and a clear proof deserves discipline, so without ijtihad and proof it is more so. But by ijtihad here is meant the meaning of scrutiny and seeking one's heart, because a layperson has no ijtihad. Then the reality of transfer is only realized in the ruling of a specific case in which he followed and acted upon it. Otherwise, his saying 'I follow Abu Hanifa in what he gave as fatwa among the issues and I commit to acting upon it in general while not knowing their specific forms' is not the reality of taqlid; rather, this is the reality of suspending taqlid or promising it, as if he committed to acting upon Abu Hanifa's statement in the issues that occur in events. If they meant this commitment, there is no evidence for the obligation of following a specific mujtahid due to his own commitment in statement or intention according to sacred law. Rather, the evidence requires acting upon the statement of the mujtahid in what he needs, by His statement: 'Ask the people of remembrance if you do not know' [An-Nahl 16:43]. Asking is only realized at the time of seeking the ruling of a specific incident. Then, when the mujtahid's statement is established with him, it is obligatory to act upon it. Mostly, such things are impositions from them to restrain people from following concessions; otherwise, the layperson would take in every matter that which is easier for him, and there is no one to prevent him. He said: (Then the reality of transfer is only realized, etc.) Al-Ramli said in Tashih al-Quduri: The usuliyyun said: It is agreed that retracting from taqlid after acting is not valid by consensus, and that is the chosen position in the madhhab. Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Khatib said in Kitab al-Fatawa: The mufti on a madhhab, if he gives a fatwa that something is such on the madhhab of an imam, he may not follow another and give a fatwa contrary to it, because it is pure imitation. He also said: By his commitment to an imam's madhhab, he is charged with it until something else appears to him, and the muqallid does not have anything appear to him. End. I say: In Al-Tahrir by Ibn al-Humam: The issue is that one does not retract in what he has followed, meaning acted upon, by agreement. Can he follow another in other matters? The chosen view is yes, because it is certain that they used to ask one mufti at one time and another at another time without committing to a single mufti. So if one commits to a specific madhhab like Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i, is he obliged to continue upon it? Some said yes. Some said no. Some said he is like one who did not commit: if he acts on a ruling by taqlid, he does not retract from it, and in other matters he may follow someone else. That is the most apparent, because there is nothing that obligates it legally, and from this derives the permissibility of his following concessions, and there is no legal impediment to it, since a person may take the lighter option if he has a way to it, provided he has not already acted on another opinion. End. Sheikh Hasan al-Sharnbulali has a treatise called 'Al-Iqd al-Farid fi Jawaz al-Taqlid' in which he mentioned that the claim of consensus on not retracting from what has been followed was mentioned by al-Amidi and Ibn al-Hajib and followed by them in Jam' al-Jawami' and others. Allama Ibn Abi Sharif mentioned that in the speech of others there is something suggesting the existence of disagreement after acting, so he may follow by the statement of another afterwards. Similarly, al-Zarkashi, Allama Ibn Amir Hajj and Sayyid Padshah in their commentaries on Al-Tahrir say that it is permissible to follow the one who permits. Also, the prohibition is not absolute because it is interpreted as when remnants of the previous act remain that lead to talfiq of the act from a composite of two madhhabs, like following al-Shafi'i in wiping part of the head and Imam Malik in the purity of the dog in one prayer; this is what the two scholars Ibn Hajar and al-Ramli mentioned in their commentaries on al-Minhaj. In the words of Ibn al-Humam there is something that indicates this in other than this interpretation, or the intended meaning of preventing retraction in what is followed by consensus is retraction in a specific object [ayn] not a specific type [jins], which is undoing what he did as a muqallid in his act as an imam, because he cannot invalidate it by confirming it, as if he ruled by it. So if he prayed Dhuhr by wiping a quarter of the head, he cannot invalidate it by his belief that wiping all is obligatory. As for if he prayed one day on a madhhab and wants to pray another day on another, it is not prohibited. End. He has expanded the discussion, so consult it. What the investigator [al-Marghinani] mentioned about the permissibility of following concessions was rejected by Ibn Hajar, who claimed it is contrary to consensus. Allama Khayr al-Din defended him in his gloss here with lengthy speech and denied the claim of consensus; consult it. Supporting his denial is what is in Sharh Ibn Amir Hajj after citing the consensus of Ibn Abd al-Barr where he said: If it is authentic, it needs an answer, and it can be said: we do not accept the authenticity of the claim of consensus, for regarding the charge of fisq against one who follows concessions, from Ahmad there are two narrations, and Qadi Abu Ya'la interpreted the narration that declares fisq as for one who does not seek interpretation and is not a muqallid. Some Hanbalis mentioned that if the evidence is strong or he is a layperson, he is not fasiq. In Rawdat al-Nawawi and its source from the narration of al-Hannati and others from Ibn Abi Huraira that he is not fasiq by it, then perhaps it is interpreted as akin to something that comes together for him such that no mujtahid has said the combination, as the author indicated. End. The author will mention from the commentator that there are two faces regarding his fisq, the more correct of which is that he is not fasiq. Allah knows best. He said: (By the statement of a mujtahid, a statement that is easier) Al-Ramli said: The sentence from Chapter on the Mufti: If there is no one else, it becomes a personal obligation upon him, and if there is someone else, it is a collective obligation. Nevertheless, it is not permissible to rush into what is not established. Conditions are: Islam of the mufti, his integrity ('adalah), so the fatwa of a fasiq is rejected, though he may act for himself according to his ijtihad. Also required are: vigilance, strong retention, and competence in ijtihad. Whoever knows one or two issues or issues with their evidences, it is not permissible for him to give fatwa on them, nor to be followed. Similarly, one who is not a mujtahid. If a mujtahid dies, his fatwa does not become void; rather, his statement is taken. Thus, whoever knows the madhhab of a mujtahid and is fully immersed in it, it is permissible for him to give fatwa according to that mujtahid's statement. He should attribute it to the madhhab if he does not know that he gives fatwa on his own. It is not permissible for one who is not fully immersed except in known issues from the madhhab. Branch: A mujtahid may not follow another mujtahid. If a new incident occurs in which he has already exerted ijtihad, he must repeat it if he forgot the evidence or if a new problem arises. Branch: Those who claim affiliation to the madhhab of an imam are either the common people: their taqlid is based on the taqlid of the deceased, which has passed; or they are mujtahids: they do not follow others. If their ijtihad agrees with their ijtihad, no problem; if it opposes sometimes. And those who have not attained the level of ijtihad but have stopped at the principles of their imam, like Malik regarding the purity of a dog in one prayer...
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses several connected rulings: the obligation of the layperson to follow a mujtahid, the permissibility of following one's heart when two mujtahids differ, the reality of taqlid and transfer between madhhabs, the issue of talfiq (combining rulings from different madhhabs), and the qualifications of a mufti. The author begins by stating that if a layperson consults two mujtahids and they differ, it is preferable (awla) for him to choose the opinion his heart inclines to, but it is also permissible to take the other. The obligation (wajib) is simply to follow some mujtahid; the layperson is not required to know which one is correct. Then the discussion turns to transferring from one madhhab to another. The author clarifies that true taqlid occurs only when one acts on a specific ruling; a general commitment to a madhhab without acting is not real taqlid but a mere promise. The text then quotes al-Ramli, who cites a claimed consensus that one cannot retract a taqlid after acting. However, later scholars like Ibn al-Humam and al-Sharnbulali nuance this: retraction is prohibited only in the same specific act (ayn), not in a new act of a different type (jins). The author then addresses the controversial issue of talfiq—combining elements from different madhhabs in one action (e.g., following al-Shafi'i on wiping part of the head and Malik on dog purity in the same prayer). He reports that the majority of Hanafis permit talfiq as long as no single mujtahid has declared the combination valid, and that claims of consensus against it are unfounded. Finally, the passage shifts to the qualifications of a mufti: Islam, integrity, vigilance, strong memory, and competence in ijtihad. Giving fatwa is a collective obligation (fard kifayah), but if no one else qualifies, it becomes an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) on the qualified person. The fatwa of a fasiq is rejected, though he may act for himself. A non-mujtahid who is well-versed in a madhhab may give fatwa by citing that madhhab. A mujtahid, however, should not follow another mujtahid blindly; he must use his own ijtihad. The entire discussion emphasizes the importance of qualified scholarship and the flexibility within the Hanafi school for the layperson to follow valid opinions, while warning against frivolous searching for dispensations (tatarruq al-rukhas).

Key Terms
تقليد (taqlid): Following a qualified mujtahid in a legal ruling without demanding knowledge of his evidence. The text distinguishes between actual taqlid (acting on a specific ruling) and a general commitment to a madhhab.
تلفيق (talfiq): Combining rulings from different madhhabs in a single act, such that no single mujtahid would approve the combination. The text discusses its permissibility, with conditions.
عين (ayn): A specific object or act. In the context of taqlid, it refers to the particular act that was performed following a mujtahid's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the obligation of a layperson when he encounters a new legal situation?
A) He must commit to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and never change.
B) He must follow the opinion of any qualified mujtahid, and it is permissible to follow a different mujtahid in another matter.
C) He must always follow the easier opinion regardless of the mujtahid's qualification.
D) He must perform ijtihad himself even if he lacks the ability.

Key Takeaway
The layperson's obligation is to follow a qualified mujtahid for each specific incident, and he may follow different mujtahids in different matters, as long as he does not retract an act already performed.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Qualifications of a Mufti and the Conditions for Issuing Fatwa
Qualifications of a mufti and conditions for issuing fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the conditions required for a mufti (Islam, integrity, vigilance, strong memory, competence in ijtihad) and explain when giving fatwa becomes a personal obligation (fard 'ayn) versus a collective obligation (fard kifayah).

Core Idea
A mufti must be a mujtahid or a fully proficient scholar of the madhhab; giving fatwa is fard kifayah but becomes fard 'ayn if no one else qualifies.

After discussing the layperson's obligation to follow a mujtahid, the passage turns to the qualifications of the mufti himself. Who is qualified to give fatwa? The author outlines essential conditions: Islam, integrity, vigilance, strong memory, and competence in ijtihad. He also addresses the situation where a non-mujtahid may still give fatwa if he has mastered the madhhab of a particular imam. This lesson clarifies the standards of expertise required in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall obligation of giving fatwa
The text states that if there is no one else, giving fatwa becomes fard 'ayn (personal obligation) on the qualified person. If there are others, it is fard kifayah (collective obligation).
This is a key principle: the duty to provide religious guidance falls on the community, but if no one steps up, it becomes individually binding.
2
List the conditions for a mufti
The text mentions: Islam, 'adalah (integrity), yaqazah (vigilance), quwwat dabt (strong memory/retention), and ahliyyat ijtihad (competence in ijtihad).
These conditions ensure the mufti is trustworthy, alert, and capable of correct legal reasoning.
3
Understand the rejection of a fasiq mufti
The fatwa of a fasiq (sinner) is not accepted for others, though he may act on his own ijtihad.
This protects the community from unreliable rulings.
4
Explore the case of a non-mujtahid mufti
If someone is not a mujtahid but has mastered the madhhab of a mujtahid (mutabahhir), he may give fatwa by transmitting that imam's rulings. He must attribute the ruling to the madhhab.
This is the later Hanafi practice: a mufti often follows the madhhab rather than doing independent ijtihad.
5
Consider the rule for mujtahids
A mujtahid may not blindly follow another mujtahid. If he forgets the evidence or a new problem arises, he must repeat his ijtihad.
This maintains the integrity of ijtihad as an independent exercise.

Book of Judiciary: From that and its errors. Based on this, if a layperson asks two jurists, meaning mujtahids, and they differ with him, the preferable course is that he takes what his heart inclines to from them. In my view, if he takes the statement of the one to whom his heart does not incline, it is permissible, because that inclination and its absence are equal. The obligation upon him is to follow a mujtahid, and he has done so; that mujtahid is either correct or mistaken. They said: The one who transfers from a madhhab to a madhhab with ijtihad and a clear proof deserves discipline, so without ijtihad and proof it is more so. But by ijtihad here is meant the meaning of scrutiny and seeking one's heart, because a layperson has no ijtihad. Then the reality of transfer is only realized in the ruling of a specific case in which he followed and acted upon it. Otherwise, his saying 'I follow Abu Hanifa in what he gave as fatwa among the issues and I commit to acting upon it in general while not knowing their specific forms' is not the reality of taqlid; rather, this is the reality of suspending taqlid or promising it, as if he committed to acting upon Abu Hanifa's statement in the issues that occur in events. If they meant this commitment, there is no evidence for the obligation of following a specific mujtahid due to his own commitment in statement or intention according to sacred law. Rather, the evidence requires acting upon the statement of the mujtahid in what he needs, by His statement: 'Ask the people of remembrance if you do not know' [An-Nahl 16:43]. Asking is only realized at the time of seeking the ruling of a specific incident. Then, when the mujtahid's statement is established with him, it is obligatory to act upon it. Mostly, such things are impositions from them to restrain people from following concessions; otherwise, the layperson would take in every matter that which is easier for him, and there is no one to prevent him. He said: (Then the reality of transfer is only realized, etc.) Al-Ramli said in Tashih al-Quduri: The usuliyyun said: It is agreed that retracting from taqlid after acting is not valid by consensus, and that is the chosen position in the madhhab. Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Khatib said in Kitab al-Fatawa: The mufti on a madhhab, if he gives a fatwa that something is such on the madhhab of an imam, he may not follow another and give a fatwa contrary to it, because it is pure imitation. He also said: By his commitment to an imam's madhhab, he is charged with it until something else appears to him, and the muqallid does not have anything appear to him. End. I say: In Al-Tahrir by Ibn al-Humam: The issue is that one does not retract in what he has followed, meaning acted upon, by agreement. Can he follow another in other matters? The chosen view is yes, because it is certain that they used to ask one mufti at one time and another at another time without committing to a single mufti. So if one commits to a specific madhhab like Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i, is he obliged to continue upon it? Some said yes. Some said no. Some said he is like one who did not commit: if he acts on a ruling by taqlid, he does not retract from it, and in other matters he may follow someone else. That is the most apparent, because there is nothing that obligates it legally, and from this derives the permissibility of his following concessions, and there is no legal impediment to it, since a person may take the lighter option if he has a way to it, provided he has not already acted on another opinion. End. Sheikh Hasan al-Sharnbulali has a treatise called 'Al-Iqd al-Farid fi Jawaz al-Taqlid' in which he mentioned that the claim of consensus on not retracting from what has been followed was mentioned by al-Amidi and Ibn al-Hajib and followed by them in Jam' al-Jawami' and others. Allama Ibn Abi Sharif mentioned that in the speech of others there is something suggesting the existence of disagreement after acting, so he may follow by the statement of another afterwards. Similarly, al-Zarkashi, Allama Ibn Amir Hajj and Sayyid Padshah in their commentaries on Al-Tahrir say that it is permissible to follow the one who permits. Also, the prohibition is not absolute because it is interpreted as when remnants of the previous act remain that lead to talfiq of the act from a composite of two madhhabs, like following al-Shafi'i in wiping part of the head and Imam Malik in the purity of the dog in one prayer; this is what the two scholars Ibn Hajar and al-Ramli mentioned in their commentaries on al-Minhaj. In the words of Ibn al-Humam there is something that indicates this in other than this interpretation, or the intended meaning of preventing retraction in what is followed by consensus is retraction in a specific object [ayn] not a specific type [jins], which is undoing what he did as a muqallid in his act as an imam, because he cannot invalidate it by confirming it, as if he ruled by it. So if he prayed Dhuhr by wiping a quarter of the head, he cannot invalidate it by his belief that wiping all is obligatory. As for if he prayed one day on a madhhab and wants to pray another day on another, it is not prohibited. End. He has expanded the discussion, so consult it. What the investigator [al-Marghinani] mentioned about the permissibility of following concessions was rejected by Ibn Hajar, who claimed it is contrary to consensus. Allama Khayr al-Din defended him in his gloss here with lengthy speech and denied the claim of consensus; consult it. Supporting his denial is what is in Sharh Ibn Amir Hajj after citing the consensus of Ibn Abd al-Barr where he said: If it is authentic, it needs an answer, and it can be said: we do not accept the authenticity of the claim of consensus, for regarding the charge of fisq against one who follows concessions, from Ahmad there are two narrations, and Qadi Abu Ya'la interpreted the narration that declares fisq as for one who does not seek interpretation and is not a muqallid. Some Hanbalis mentioned that if the evidence is strong or he is a layperson, he is not fasiq. In Rawdat al-Nawawi and its source from the narration of al-Hannati and others from Ibn Abi Huraira that he is not fasiq by it, then perhaps it is interpreted as akin to something that comes together for him such that no mujtahid has said the combination, as the author indicated. End. The author will mention from the commentator that there are two faces regarding his fisq, the more correct of which is that he is not fasiq. Allah knows best. He said: (By the statement of a mujtahid, a statement that is easier) Al-Ramli said: The sentence from Chapter on the Mufti: If there is no one else, it becomes a personal obligation upon him, and if there is someone else, it is a collective obligation. Nevertheless, it is not permissible to rush into what is not established. Conditions are: Islam of the mufti, his integrity ('adalah), so the fatwa of a fasiq is rejected, though he may act for himself according to his ijtihad. Also required are: vigilance, strong retention, and competence in ijtihad. Whoever knows one or two issues or issues with their evidences, it is not permissible for him to give fatwa on them, nor to be followed. Similarly, one who is not a mujtahid. If a mujtahid dies, his fatwa does not become void; rather, his statement is taken. Thus, whoever knows the madhhab of a mujtahid and is fully immersed in it, it is permissible for him to give fatwa according to that mujtahid's statement. He should attribute it to the madhhab if he does not know that he gives fatwa on his own. It is not permissible for one who is not fully immersed except in known issues from the madhhab. Branch: A mujtahid may not follow another mujtahid. If a new incident occurs in which he has already exerted ijtihad, he must repeat it if he forgot the evidence or if a new problem arises. Branch: Those who claim affiliation to the madhhab of an imam are either the common people: their taqlid is based on the taqlid of the deceased, which has passed; or they are mujtahids: they do not follow others. If their ijtihad agrees with their ijtihad, no problem; if it opposes sometimes. And those who have not attained the level of ijtihad but have stopped at the principles of their imam, like Malik regarding the purity of a dog in one prayer...
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses several connected rulings: the obligation of the layperson to follow a mujtahid, the permissibility of following one's heart when two mujtahids differ, the reality of taqlid and transfer between madhhabs, the issue of talfiq (combining rulings from different madhhabs), and the qualifications of a mufti. The author begins by stating that if a layperson consults two mujtahids and they differ, it is preferable (awla) for him to choose the opinion his heart inclines to, but it is also permissible to take the other. The obligation (wajib) is simply to follow some mujtahid; the layperson is not required to know which one is correct. Then the discussion turns to transferring from one madhhab to another. The author clarifies that true taqlid occurs only when one acts on a specific ruling; a general commitment to a madhhab without acting is not real taqlid but a mere promise. The text then quotes al-Ramli, who cites a claimed consensus that one cannot retract a taqlid after acting. However, later scholars like Ibn al-Humam and al-Sharnbulali nuance this: retraction is prohibited only in the same specific act (ayn), not in a new act of a different type (jins). The author then addresses the controversial issue of talfiq—combining elements from different madhhabs in one action (e.g., following al-Shafi'i on wiping part of the head and Malik on dog purity in the same prayer). He reports that the majority of Hanafis permit talfiq as long as no single mujtahid has declared the combination valid, and that claims of consensus against it are unfounded. Finally, the passage shifts to the qualifications of a mufti: Islam, integrity, vigilance, strong memory, and competence in ijtihad. Giving fatwa is a collective obligation (fard kifayah), but if no one else qualifies, it becomes an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) on the qualified person. The fatwa of a fasiq is rejected, though he may act for himself. A non-mujtahid who is well-versed in a madhhab may give fatwa by citing that madhhab. A mujtahid, however, should not follow another mujtahid blindly; he must use his own ijtihad. The entire discussion emphasizes the importance of qualified scholarship and the flexibility within the Hanafi school for the layperson to follow valid opinions, while warning against frivolous searching for dispensations (tatarruq al-rukhas).

Key Terms
مفتي (mufti): One who gives legal rulings (fatwa). The mufti must be a mujtahid or, in later Hanafi practice, a fully proficient scholar of the madhhab (mutabahhir).
تبحر (tabahhur): Deep immersion and mastery in a madhhab, enabling a non-mujtahid to transmit rulings accurately.
فرض كفاية (fard kifayah): A collective obligation: if some members of the community fulfill it, the rest are absolved; if none fulfill it, all are sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is giving fatwa a personal obligation (fard 'ayn)?
A) Always, because every qualified person must give fatwa.
B) Only when there is no other qualified person available.
C) Only for matters of worship, not transactions.
D) It is never a personal obligation; it is always a collective obligation.

Key Takeaway
Giving fatwa is a collective obligation that becomes personally binding on the qualified mufti only when no one else can do it; the mufti must meet conditions of Islam, integrity, vigilance, strong memory, and ijtihad competence, and a non-mujtahid may serve if he has fully mastered the madhhab.

Qualifications and Obligation of the Mufti
States the obligation and conditions of giving fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the obligation of giving fatwa and identify the required conditions for a mufti.

Core Idea
Fatwa is a collective duty that becomes personal when no other qualified mufti exists.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq establishes the foundational rules for issuing fatwa. It clarifies when giving fatwa is fard kifayah and when it becomes fard 'ayn, and it lists the essential conditions a mufti must meet for his fatwa to be valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on giving fatwa
Determine whether the obligation is fard kifayah or fard 'ayn based on the presence of other muftis.
The text states: 'فإن لم يكن غيره تعين عليه وإن كان غيره فهو فرض كفاية'.
2
List the conditions of a mufti
Extract the conditions mentioned: Islam, 'adalah, alertness, strong memory, and qualification for ijtihad.
The condition of 'adalah means the mufti must be upright and not a fasiq.
3
Explain the consequence of missing conditions
If the mufti is a fasiq, his fatwa is rejected; if he lacks ijtihad, he cannot give fatwa except in known issues.
The text says: 'ترد فتوى الفاسق' and 'من لم يكن مجتهدا لا تجوز فتواه'.
4
Note the permissibility of following a dead mujtahid
The fatwa of a deceased mujtahid remains valid and can be followed.
This is the basis for taqlid of a dead scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة بقول مجتهد قوله أخف عليه، وأنا لا أدري ما يمنع هذا من النقل أو العقل وكون ما الإنسان يتبع هو . أخف على نفسه من قول مجتهد سوغ له الاجتهاد وما علمت من الشرع ذمه عليه وكان الله يحب ما خفف عن أمته ؛ إلى هنا ما في فتح القدير. ولم يبسط أصحابنا الكلام على المفتي والمستفتي في المتون والشروح وإنما ذكر أصحاب الفتاوى بعض مسائلهما، وقد بسط الكلام عليهما في الروض في كتاب القضاء فأحببت نقله لأن قواعدنا لا تأباه ثم أنبه بعده على نقل البعض لمذهبنا والله تعالى أعلم قال :
فصل في المفتي : فإن لم يكن غيره تعين عليه وإن كان غيره فهو فرض كفاية، ومع هذا لا يحل التسارع إلى ما لا يتحقق ويشترط إسلام المفتي وعدالته فترد فتوى الفاسق ويعمل لنفسه باجتهاده ويشترط تيقظه وقوة ضبطه وأهلية اجتهاده، فمن عرف مسألة أو مسألتين أو مسائل بأدلتها لم تجز فتواه بها ولا تقليده وكذا من لم يكن مجتهداً. ولو مات المجتهد لم تبطل فتواه بل يؤخذ بقوله، فعلى هذا من عرف مذهب مجتهد وتبحر فيه جاز أن يفتي بقول ذلك المجتهد . وليضف إلى المذهب إن لم يعلم أنه يفتي عليه ولا يجوز لغير المتبحر إلا في مسائل معلومة من المذهب فرع ليس لمجتهد تقليد مجتهد ولو حدثت واقعة قد اجتهد فيها وجب إعادته إن نسي الدليل أو تجدد مشكل فرع المنتسبون إلى مذهب إمام إما عوام فتقليدهم مفرع على تقليد الميت فقد مر، وإما مجتهدون فلا يقلدون، فإن وافق اجتهاده اجتهادهم فلا بأس وإن خالفه أحياناً، ومن لم يبلغ رتبة الاجتهاد بل وقف على أصول إمامه مالك في طهارة الكلب في صلاة واحدة؛ كذا ذكر العلمتان ابن حجر والرملي في شرحهما على المنهاج. وفي كلام ابن الهمام ما يفيد ذلك في غير هذا الحمل أو المراد بمنع المرجوع فيما قلد فيه اتفاقاً الرجوع في خصوص العين لا خصوص الجنس، وذلك بنقض ما فعله مقلداً في فعله إماماً لأنه لا يملك إبطاله بإمضائه كما لو قضى به فلو صلى ظهراً بمسح ربع الرأس ليس له إبطالها باعتقاده لزوم مسح الكل، وأما لو صلى يوماً على مذهب وأراد أن يصلي يوماً آخر على غيره فلا يمنع منه اهـ. وقد بسط الكلام فيها فراجعه وما ذكره المحقق من جواز تتبع الرخص رده ابن حجر وزعم أنه مخالف للإجماع، وانتصر له العلامة خير الدين في حاشيته هنا بكلام طويل ومنع دعوى الإجماع فراجعه ويؤيد منعه ما في شرح ابن أمير حاج بعد نقله الإجماع عن ابن عبد البر حيث قال : إن صح احتاج إلى جواب ويمكن أن يقال لا نسلم صحة دعوى الإجماع إذ في تفسيق المتتبع للرخص عن أحمد روايتان، وحمل القاضي أبو يعلى الرواية المفسقة على غير متأول ولا مقلد، وذكر بعض الحنابلة إن قوي دليل أو كان عامياً لا يفسق وفي روضة النووي وأصلها عن حكاية الحناطي وغيره عن ابن أبي هريرة أنه لا يفسق به ثم لعله محمول على نحو ما يجتمع له من ذلك ما لم يقل بمجموعه مجتهد كما أشار إليه المصنف .اهـ. وسيذكر المؤلف عن الشارح أن في فسقه وجهين أوجههما عدمه والله سبحانه أعلم. قوله : (بقول مجتهد قول أخف) قال الرملي: الجملة من وتمكن من قياس ما لم ينص عليه على المنصوص فليس بمقلد في نفسه بل هو واسطة، فإن نص صاحب المذهب على الحكم والعلة الحق بها غير المنصوص، ولو نص على الحكم فقط فله أن يستنبط العلة ويقيس وليقل هذا قياس مذهبه لا ،قوله وإن اختلف نص إمامه في مشتبهين فله التخريج من أحدهما إلى الأخرى. فرع للمفتي أن يغلظ للزجر متأولاً كما إذا قتله وخشي أن يقتله جاز أن يقول إن قتلته قتلناك متأولاً لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «من من له عبد عن قتل عبده قتلناه» (۱) وهذا إذا لم يترتب على إطلاقه مفسدة واختلاف المفتين كالمجتهدين والله تعالى أعلم .
Issue: With the statement of a mujtahid, his statement is lighter upon him. I do not know what prevents this from transmission or reason, and that a person follows what is lighter on himself from the statement of a mujtahid for whom ijtihad is permissible, and I do not know from the Shari'ah any blame upon him, and Allah loves what He made light upon His Ummah; up to here is what is in Fath al-Qadir. Our scholars did not elaborate on the mufti and mustafti in the matn and sharh; rather, the authors of fatwas mentioned some of their issues. And the discussion on them has been elaborated in al-Rawd in the Book of Judiciary, so I liked to quote it because our principles do not reject it, then I will point out later the transmission of some for our madhhab, and Allah knows best. He said: Chapter on the Mufti: If there is no other, it becomes personally obligatory upon him; but if there are others, it is a collective obligation. Yet with this, it is not permissible to rush to what is not verified. The conditions are the mufti's Islam and uprightness (adalah), so the fatwa of a corrupt person is rejected, but he acts according to his own ijtihad for himself. Also required are his alertness, strength of memory, and qualification for ijtihad. Whoever knows one or two issues or issues with their proofs, his fatwa on them and taqlid of him are not permissible, and likewise one who is not a mujtahid. If a mujtahid dies, his fatwa does not become invalid; rather his opinion is followed. Based on this, whoever knows the madhhab of a mujtahid and is deeply learned in it may give fatwa according to that mujtahid's opinion. He should attribute it to the madhhab if he does not know that he is giving fatwa based on it. It is not permissible for one who is not deeply learned except in issues known from the madhhab. Branch: It is not for a mujtahid to imitate another mujtahid. If an incident arises in which he has already exerted ijtihad, he must do it again if he forgot the evidence, or if a new problem arises. Branch: Those who attribute themselves to the madhhab of an imam are either laymen, so their taqlid is based on taqlid of a dead person as has passed, or mujtahids, so they do not imitate. If his ijtihad agrees with their ijtihad, there is no problem; if it differs sometimes... And whoever has not reached the level of ijtihad but has stopped at the principles of his imam, ... Malik on the purity of the dog in one prayer? Thus mentioned the two scholars Ibn Hajar and al-Ramli in their commentaries on al-Minhaj. In the words of Ibn al-Humam, that indicates... in other than this interpretation, or what is meant by prohibiting return in what one has imitated, by consensus, is a return with respect to the specific individual, not the specific type... And he elaborated on it, so refer to it. What the muhaqqiq mentioned about the permissibility of following concessions (tashaffur) was rejected by Ibn Hajar, who claimed it is contrary to consensus. 'Allamah Khayr al-Din defended him in his marginalia with lengthy speech and denied the claim of consensus, so refer to it. Supporting his denial is what is in Sharh Ibn Amir Hajj after he transmitted consensus from Ibn 'Abd al-Barr, where he said: If it is authentic, it requires an answer, and it can be said: We do not accept the claim of consensus, because regarding declaring the follower of concessions as sinful, from Ahmad there are two narrations. Qadi Abu Ya'la interpreted the narration that declares sinfulness as referring to one who is not muta'awwil (interpretive) and not a muqallid. Some Hanbalis mentioned that if the evidence is strong or he is a layman, he is not sinful. In Rawdat al-Nawawi and its source from the report of al-Hannati and others from Ibn Abi Hurayrah, that he is not sinful thereby. Then perhaps it is construed to mean something like what is gathered for him so long as he does not follow a combination that no single mujtahid has said, as the author indicated. End. The author will mention from the commentator that there are two opinions on his being sinful, the strongest being that he is not sinful, and Allah knows best. His statement: (By the statement of a mujtahid, his statement is lighter), al-Ramli said: the sentence is from... And he is able to analogize what is not mentioned to what is mentioned, so he is not a muqallid in himself but rather an intermediary. If the imam of the madhhab has stated the ruling and the cause, he attaches what is not mentioned to it through the cause. If he stated only the ruling, he may derive the cause and analogize, and he should say this is the qiyas of his madhhab, not his own statement. If the texts of his imam differ in two similar issues, he may derive (takhrij) from one to the other. Branch: For the mufti, it is permissible to be stern for deterrent purposes, interpretively, such as if someone killed and he fears he will kill, he may say, 'If you kill him, we will kill you,' interpretively, based on the Prophet's saying: 'Whoever...' (Hadith). This is if no harm results from its absolute expression. The disagreement of muftis is like the disagreement of mujtahids, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement concerning the qualifications and obligations of a mufti. It begins by discussing the issue of following a mujtahid's lighter opinion, then quotes from al-Rawd. The author states that giving fatwa is a collective obligation (fard kifayah); if no one else can do it, it becomes personally obligatory (fard 'ayn). The mufti must be Muslim, upright ('adl), alert, with strong memory, and qualified for ijtihad. The fatwa of a corrupt person (fasiq) is rejected. One who only knows a few issues with their proofs cannot give fatwa or be imitated. The passage discusses the death of a mujtahid, the permissibility of giving fatwa based on his madhhab, and the requirement to attribute the fatwa to the madhhab. It also addresses the issue of a mujtahid imitating another mujtahid and the obligations when a similar incident occurs. The later parts discuss the detailed rules for those who are not mujtahids but are followers of a madhhab, including the concept of 'wasta' (intermediary) who can perform qiyas within the madhhab. The passage also allows a mufti to use stern language for deterrence. The discussion on 'tashaffur' (following concessions) is also included, with citations from later scholars. All rulings are faithful to the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
مفتي (mufti): One who gives fatwa; must be a mujtahid or a deeply learned follower of a madhhab capable of qiyas within the madhhab.
مجتهد (mujtahid): One who performs ijtihad; the text distinguishes between absolute mujtahid and one who follows a madhhab.
تقليد (taqlid): Following the opinion of a mujtahid without evidence; the text addresses taqlid of a dead mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the fatwa of a corrupt (fasiq) mufti according to the text?
A) It is valid but disliked.
B) It is rejected and not accepted.
C) It is accepted only if correct.
D) It is valid for himself but not others.

Key Takeaway
Giving fatwa is a collective obligation with strict conditions; only a qualified mujtahid or a deeply learned follower can issue valid fatwas.

Taqlid of a Dead Mujtahid and the Intermediary Role
Expands on taqlid and tashaffur rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi stance on taqlid of a deceased mujtahid and identify the role of a wasta, and evaluate the dispute on following concessions (tashaffur).

Core Idea
A dead mujtahid's fatwa remains valid, and a deeply learned follower may act as an intermediary to issue fatwa.

This section of the passage delves into the rules of taqlid after a mujtahid's death and the qualifications of those who are not mujtahids but attribute to a madhhab. It also addresses the controversial issue of following concessions and when it is permissible.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on taqlid of a dead mujtahid
The text affirms that the fatwa of a deceased mujtahid does not become invalid; it can still be followed.
Text: 'ولو مات المجتهد لم تبطل فتواه بل يؤخذ بقوله'.
2
Define the role of the wasta (intermediary)
A deeply learned non-mujtahid who knows the madhhab's principles and can analogize within the madhhab may issue fatwa, attributing it to the madhhab.
The text says: 'من عرف مذهب مجتهد وتبحر فيه جاز أن يفتي بقول ذلك المجتهد وليضف إلى المذهب'.
3
Explain the rule for a mujtahid
A mujtahid cannot imitate another mujtahid; he must follow his own ijtihad.
Text: 'فرع ليس لمجتهد تقليد مجتهد'.
4
Analyze the dispute on tashaffur (following concessions)
The text presents the disagreement: some scholars declare it sinful, others permit it under conditions. The Hanafi position leans toward permissibility if not leading to a combination no single mujtahid approved.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the mufta bihi is that it is not sinful if done with proper taqlid.

The conditions are the mufti's Islam and uprightness (adalah), so the fatwa of a corrupt person is rejected, but he acts according to his own ijtihad for himself. Also required are his alertness, strength of memory, and qualification for ijtihad. Whoever knows one or two issues or issues with their proofs, his fatwa on them and taqlid of him are not permissible, and likewise one who is not a mujtahid. If a mujtahid dies, his fatwa does not become invalid; rather his opinion is followed. Based on this, whoever knows the madhhab of a mujtahid and is deeply learned in it may give fatwa according to that mujtahid's opinion. He should attribute it to the madhhab if he does not know that he is giving fatwa based on it. It is not permissible for one who is not deeply learned except in issues known from the madhhab. Branch: It is not for a mujtahid to imitate another mujtahid. If an incident arises in which he has already exerted ijtihad, he must do it again if he forgot the evidence, or if a new problem arises. Branch: Those who attribute themselves to the madhhab of an imam are either laymen, so their taqlid is based on taqlid of a dead person as has passed, or mujtahids, so they do not imitate. If his ijtihad agrees with their ijtihad, there is no problem; if it differs sometimes... And whoever has not reached the level of ijtihad but has stopped at the principles of his imam, ... Malik on the purity of the dog in one prayer? Thus mentioned the two scholars Ibn Hajar and al-Ramli in their commentaries on al-Minhaj. In the words of Ibn al-Humam, that indicates... in other than this interpretation, or what is meant by prohibiting return in what one has imitated, by consensus, is a return with respect to the specific individual, not the specific type... And he elaborated on it, so refer to it. What the muhaqqiq mentioned about the permissibility of following concessions (tashaffur) was rejected by Ibn Hajar, who claimed it is contrary to consensus. 'Allamah Khayr al-Din defended him in his marginalia with lengthy speech and denied the claim of consensus, so refer to it. Supporting his denial is what is in Sharh Ibn Amir Hajj after he transmitted consensus from Ibn 'Abd al-Barr, where he said: If it is authentic, it requires an answer, and it can be said: We do not accept the claim of consensus, because regarding declaring the follower of concessions as sinful, from Ahmad there are two narrations. Qadi Abu Ya'la interpreted the narration that declares sinfulness as referring to one who is not muta'awwil (interpretive) and not a muqallid. Some Hanbalis mentioned that if the evidence is strong or he is a layman, he is not sinful. In Rawdat al-Nawawi and its source from the report of al-Hannati and others from Ibn Abi Hurayrah, that he is not sinful thereby. Then perhaps it is construed to mean something like what is gathered for him so long as he does not follow a combination that no single mujtahid has said, as the author indicated. End. The author will mention from the commentator that there are two opinions on his being sinful, the strongest being that he is not sinful, and Allah knows best. His statement: (By the statement of a mujtahid, his statement is lighter), al-Ramli said: the sentence is from... And he is able to analogize what is not mentioned to what is mentioned, so he is not a muqallid in himself but rather an intermediary. If the imam of the madhhab has stated the ruling and the cause, he attaches what is not mentioned to it through the cause. If he stated only the ruling, he may derive the cause and analogize, and he should say this is the qiyas of his madhhab, not his own statement. If the texts of his imam differ in two similar issues, he may derive (takhrij) from one to the other. Branch: For the mufti, it is permissible to be stern for deterrent purposes, interpretively, such as if someone killed and he fears he will kill, he may say, 'If you kill him, we will kill you,' interpretively, based on the Prophet's saying: 'Whoever...' (Hadith). This is if no harm results from its absolute expression. The disagreement of muftis is like the disagreement of mujtahids, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement concerning the qualifications and obligations of a mufti. It begins by discussing the issue of following a mujtahid's lighter opinion, then quotes from al-Rawd. The author states that giving fatwa is a collective obligation (fard kifayah); if no one else can do it, it becomes personally obligatory (fard 'ayn). The mufti must be Muslim, upright ('adl), alert, with strong memory, and qualified for ijtihad. The fatwa of a corrupt person (fasiq) is rejected. One who only knows a few issues with their proofs cannot give fatwa or be imitated. The passage discusses the death of a mujtahid, the permissibility of giving fatwa based on his madhhab, and the requirement to attribute the fatwa to the madhhab. It also addresses the issue of a mujtahid imitating another mujtahid and the obligations when a similar incident occurs. The later parts discuss the detailed rules for those who are not mujtahids but are followers of a madhhab, including the concept of 'wasta' (intermediary) who can perform qiyas within the madhhab. The passage also allows a mufti to use stern language for deterrence. The discussion on 'tashaffur' (following concessions) is also included, with citations from later scholars. All rulings are faithful to the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
مفتي (mufti): One who gives fatwa; must be a mujtahid or a deeply learned follower of a madhhab capable of qiyas within the madhhab.
مجتهد (mujtahid): One who performs ijtihad; the text distinguishes between absolute mujtahid and one who follows a madhhab.
تقليد (taqlid): Following the opinion of a mujtahid without evidence; the text addresses taqlid of a dead mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must a deeply learned follower (wasta) do when issuing a fatwa?
A) Issue the fatwa as his own opinion.
B) Attribute the fatwa to his personal ijtihad.
C) Attribute the fatwa to the madhhab of his imam.
D) Cite the evidence directly.

Key Takeaway
A dead mujtahid's opinion remains authoritative, and a learned follower may serve as an intermediary, but must attribute to the madhhab. Tashaffur is permissible within limits.

Obligation to Seek a Fatwa and Weighing Muftis
Obligations and conditions for the mustafti in seeking a fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation of seeking a fatwa, identify the conditions for a mufti's qualification, and apply the Hanafi rule for preferring one mufti over another when they differ.

Core Idea
Seeking a fatwa is wajib from a mufti known for knowledge and uprightness; if muftis differ, follow the most knowledgeable.

This passage opens the chapter on the mustafti, defining the essential obligation to seek fatwa only from a qualified mufti. It explains how to verify a mufti's qualifications and what to do when multiple scholars give conflicting answers. The rulings here are foundational for anyone needing a fatwa, ensuring that the seeker acts on sound authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation
Find the sentence stating that it is wajib (obligatory) to seek a fatwa from someone whose knowledge and uprightness are known.
The verb 'yajib' indicates obligation.
2
Determine methods of verification
Identify the two acceptable methods: report of a trustworthy person or widespread reputation (istifādah). If inner uprightness is hidden, external uprightness suffices.
3
Apply precedence when muftis differ
If two muftis give different answers and there is no clear text (nass), the mustafti must follow the more knowledgeable (a‘lam). If he believes one is more knowledgeable and another more pious, knowledge takes precedence.
The maxim 'al-a‘lam muqaddam ‘alā al-awra‘' is applied here.
4
Note the exception for ignorance
The text says 'he acts upon the fatwa of a scholar even if there is one more knowledgeable, out of ignorance.' This means if the mustafti does not know who is more knowledgeable, he may follow any qualified scholar.
Ignorance here refers to the mustafti's lack of knowledge about relative superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المستفتي : يجب أن يستفتي من عرف علمه وعدالته ولو بإخبار ثقة عارف أو باستفاضة وإلا بحث عن ذلك فلو خفيت عدالته الباطنة اكتفى بالعدالة الظاهرة. ويعمل بفتوى عالم مع وجود أعلم جهله فإن اختلفا ولا نص قدم الأعلم، وكذا إذا اعتقد أحدهما أعلم أو أورع ويقدم الأعلم على الأورع ولو أجيب في واقعة لا تتكرر ثم حدثت لزم إعادة السؤال إن لم يعلم استناد الجواب إلى نص أو إجماع، وإن لم تطمئن نفسه إلى جواب المفتي استحب سؤال غيره ولا يجب. ويكفي المستفتي بعث رقعة أو رسول ثقة، ومن الأدب أن لا يسأل والمفتي قائم أو مشغول بما يمنع تمام الفكر وأن لا يقول بجوابه هكذا قلت أنا ولا يطالبه بدليل فإن أراده فوقت آخر وليبين موضع السؤال وينقط المشتبه في الرقعة ويتأملها لا سيما آخرها، ويتثبت ولا يقدح الإسراع مع التحقيق وأن يشاور فيما يحسن اظهاره من حضر متأهلاً وإن يصلح لحناً فاحشاً، وليشغل بياضاً بخط كيلا يلحق بشيء ويبين خطه بقلم بين قلمين، ولا بأس بكتبه الدليل لا السؤال، ولا يكتب خلف يصلح، وله أن من يضرب عليه إن أمن فتنة وإن سخط المالك وينهي المستفتي عن ذلك، وليس له الرقعة، وينبغي للإمام أن يبحث عن أهل العلم عمن يصلح للفتوى ليمنع من لا يصلح، وليكن المفتي متنزهاً عن خوارم المروءة فقيه النفس سليم الذهن حسن التصرف ولو عبداً أو امرأة أو أخرس تفهم إشارته، وليس هو كالشاهد في رد فتواه لقرابة وجر نفع وتقبل فتوى لا يكفر، ولا يفسق ببدعة كشهادته ويفتي ولو كان قاضياً وفي اشتراط معرفة الحساب لتصحيح مسائله وجهان ويشترط أن يحفظ مذهب إمامه ويعرف قواعده وأساليبه. وليس المبتدأ والخبر نعت لمجتهد قوله : ( ويكره أن يقتصر على ما فيه (قولان) أي على قوله في الجواب فيه قولان .
Chapter on the Mustafti: It is obligatory that he seek a fatwa from one whose knowledge and uprightness are known, even if through the report of a trustworthy knowledgeable person or through widespread reputation. Otherwise, he investigates. If the inner uprightness is hidden, external uprightness suffices. He acts upon the fatwa of a scholar even if there is one more knowledgeable, out of ignorance. If they differ and there is no text, he gives precedence to the more knowledgeable. Likewise, if he believes one is more knowledgeable or more pious, he gives precedence to the more knowledgeable over the more pious. If an answer was given for an incident that does not recur, then it occurs again, renewing the question is necessary if he does not know that the answer is based on a text or consensus. If his soul is not at ease with the mufti's answer, it is recommended to ask another, not obligatory. It suffices for the mustafti to send a paper or a trustworthy messenger. From good manners: not to ask while the mufti is standing or busy with something that prevents complete thought; not to say regarding his answer 'thus I say'; not to demand evidence from him; if he wants it, then at another time; to clarify the point of the question; to dot the ambiguous parts in the paper; to ponder it especially its end; to be thorough; speed with verification is not objectionable; to consult with those present who are qualified regarding what is appropriate to display; to correct a glaring grammatical mistake; to fill the margins with writing so that nothing gets attached; to make his handwriting clear with a pen between two pens; there is no harm in writing the evidence but not the question; not to write behind something that can be amended; he may strike out (the answer) if he is safe from fitna even if the owner is angry; the mustafti should be forbidden from that; the questioner does not have the paper. It is incumbent upon the ruler (imam) to search among the people of knowledge for those fit to give fatwa, to prevent those who are not fit. The mufti should be free from things that diminish his honor, deeply understanding, sound-minded, good in conduct, even if he is a slave, a woman, or a mute who communicates by signs. He is not like the witness; his fatwa is not rejected due to kinship or benefit; the fatwa of one who does not commit kufr is accepted; he does not become a sinner due to a bid'ah (innovation) like his testimony, and he gives fatwa even if he is a judge. There are two opinions on stipulating knowledge of arithmetic to correct his issues. He must memorize the madhhab of his imam and know its principles and methods. And 'al-mubtada' and al-khabar' is a description of a mujtahid. His statement: (It is disliked to limit oneself to what has two opinions) meaning to limit to his statement in the answer where there are two opinions.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement concerning the mustafti (the person seeking a fatwa). It begins by stating that it is obligatory (yajib) to seek a fatwa from a mufti whose knowledge and uprightness (adalah) are known. The knowledge of the mufti's qualification can be obtained through trustworthy reports or widespread reputation. If the inner uprightness is hidden, external uprightness is sufficient. The mustafti should act upon the fatwa of a knowledgeable scholar even if a more knowledgeable one exists, due to his ignorance. If two scholars differ and there is no clear text, he should follow the more knowledgeable. If he believes one is more knowledgeable or more pious, the more knowledgeable is preferred over the more pious. If a fatwa was given for a non-recurring incident, then it occurs again, he must ask again unless he knows the answer is based on text or consensus. If he is not satisfied with the answer, it is recommended to ask another, not obligatory. The passage then details the etiquette of asking: sending a paper or messenger, not asking while the mufti is busy, not claiming the answer as one's own, not demanding evidence immediately, being clear and precise in the question, and so on. The ruler (imam) should ensure only qualified persons give fatwa. The mufti should be of good character and conduct, even if a slave, woman, or mute. His fatwa is not rejected due to kinship or benefit. There is a discussion about the mufti's knowledge of arithmetic and the need to know the madhhab's principles. The passage ends with a note that it is disliked to limit oneself to what has two opinions in the answer.

Key Terms
مستفتي (mustafti): The person who seeks a fatwa; must fulfill conditions like verifying the mufti's qualifications.
رقعة (ruq'ah): A paper on which the question is written to the mufti; it is sufficient for the mustafti to send one.
عدالة ظاهرة (ʻadālah ẓāhirah): External uprightness; if inner uprightness is hidden, this is sufficient for the mustafti.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should the mustafti do if two muftis differ and there is no clear text (nass)?
A) He should follow the more pious one.
B) He should follow the more knowledgeable one.
C) He should choose the lighter opinion.
D) He may ask a third mufti.

Key Takeaway
The mustafti must seek a fatwa from a qualified mufti; when muftis differ without text, the more knowledgeable is preferred over the more pious.

Etiquette and Practical Rules for the Mustafti
Etiquette and specific rulings for the mustafti in the process of seeking and receiving fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the recommended etiquette for seeking a fatwa, determine when renewal of a question is obligatory, and apply the rules regarding sending a ruq'ah (written question) and seeking a second opinion.

Core Idea
Etiquette of seeking fatwa includes not disturbing the mufti, not claiming the answer as one's own, and renewing the question if the incident recurs without known textual basis.

After establishing the obligation to seek a fatwa, the passage details numerous rules of conduct for the mustafti. These cover how to present the question, how to behave while awaiting the answer, and what to do if not satisfied with the ruling. The adab (etiquette) ensures the process respects both the mufti's time and the integrity of the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify renewal obligation
Find the condition under which the mustafti must renew the question for a recurring incident: if the original answer was not known to be based on a text or consensus.
The ruling is expressed with 'lazima iʻādat al-suʼāl' (renewing the question is necessary).
2
Classify the second opinion rule
Locate the phrase about being at ease: if the mustafti's soul is not at ease, it is recommended (mustahabb) to ask another mufti, not obligatory.
This clarifies the earlier mention of ikhtilaf about seeking a second fatwa.
3
List the etiquettes of asking
Scan the text for the prohibitions and recommendations: not asking while the mufti is standing or busy, not saying 'thus I say', not demanding evidence immediately, clarifying the question, dotting ambiguous letters, pondering the paper, being thorough, consulting qualified attendees, correcting glaring mistakes, filling margins, making clear handwriting, writing evidence but not the question, and not writing on the back of something that can be amended.
4
Understand the ruq'ah (paper) rules
The mustafti may send a ruq'ah or a trustworthy messenger. The mufti keeps the paper; the mustafti does not have the right to it. The mufti may strike out the answer if safe from fitna even if the owner is angry.
5
Note the ruler's duty and mufti's qualifications
The imam (ruler) must search for qualified persons and prevent unqualified ones. The mufti must be free from things that diminish his honor, deep understanding, sound mind, good conduct, even if a slave, woman, or mute. His fatwa is not rejected due to kinship or benefit.
This also touches on the mufti's qualifications as relevant to the mustafti.

Mufti's Qualifications and Use of Intimidation (Taghliz)
Opening of Kitab al-Qada' discussing mufti's role as mujtahid and a branch on intimidation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the level of ijtihad required for a Hanafi mufti, explain how he performs qiyas and takhrij, and identify the conditions for using taghliz in fatwa.

Core Idea
A Hanafi mufti must be a mujtahid fi al-madhhab and may use taghliz only interpretively without causing harm.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq opens Kitab al-Qada' by clarifying the mufti's role: he is not a mere muqallid but must be capable of qiyas and takhrij within the madhhab. It then presents a branch (far') permitting the use of harsh language (taghliz) as a deterrent, provided it is done interpretively and does not cause harm, supported by a Prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mufti's status
The text says he is not a muqallid but an intermediary (wasiṭa). He must be able to analogize unstated cases to stated ones.
2
Understand qiyas method
If the imam states both ruling and 'illa, the mufti applies it. If only ruling, he deduces the 'illa and performs qiyas, attributing the result to the madhhab.
3
Apply takhrij
When the imam's texts differ on similar cases, the mufti extracts one from the other to resolve the conflict.
4
Analyze the branch on taghliz
The mufti may use harsh language for deterrence, but only if he is interpreting (mutawwil) and no corruption results. The hadith about threatening qisas for killing a slave is cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتمكن من قياس ما لم ينص عليه على المنصوص فليس بمقلد في نفسه بل هو واسطة، فإن نص صاحب المذهب على الحكم والعلة الحق بها غير المنصوص، ولو نص على الحكم فقط فله أن يستنبط العلة ويقيس وليقل هذا قياس مذهبه لا ،قوله وإن اختلف نص إمامه في مشتبهين فله التخريج من أحدهما إلى الأخرى. فرع للمفتي أن يغلظ للزجر متأولاً كما إذا قتله وخشي أن يقتله جاز أن يقول إن قتلته قتلناك متأولاً لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «من من له عبد عن قتل عبده قتلناه» (۱) وهذا إذا لم يترتب على إطلاقه مفسدة واختلاف المفتين كالمجتهدين والله تعالى أعلم
And he is capable of analogizing what is not explicitly stated to what is explicitly stated, so he is not a mere follower but an intermediary. If the author of the madhhab explicitly states the ruling and the effective cause, he links the unstated case to it by that cause. And if he only states the ruling, then he may deduce the cause and analogize, and he should say this is a conclusion of his madhhab, not his own saying. And if the texts of his imam differ on two ambiguous cases, he may extract from one to the other. Branch: The mufti may use harshness for deterrence by way of interpretation, such as if someone killed and he fears that he will kill, he may say 'If you kill, we will kill you' by way of interpretation, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever has a slave and kills his slave, we will kill him.' And this is if no harm results from its utterance. And the differences of muftis are like those of mujtahids. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is from the opening of Kitab al-Qada' in Bahr al-Raiq. It first explains the level of ijtihad required for a mufti: he must be a mujtahid fi al-madhhab, meaning he can extend the imam's rulings to new cases by identifying the effective cause ('illa). If the imam stated both hukm and 'illa, the mufti simply applies it; if only the hukm, he deduces the 'illa and performs qiyas, but must attribute the result to the madhhab, not his own opinion. If the imam's texts conflict on similar cases, he performs takhrij to harmonize. Then a branch (far') states that the mufti may use intimidating language (taghliz) as a deterrent, provided it is interpretive (i.e., based on a legal principle) and does not cause corruption. The hadith cited (from Abu Dawud, etc.) supports threatening retribution. Finally, differences among muftis are treated like differences among mujtahids—each may be correct in his ijtihad.

Key Terms
مجتهد (mujtahid): A scholar capable of deriving rulings from primary sources; here specifically a mujtahid within the madhhab who can analogize from the imam's rulings.
تقليد (taqlid): Following an imam without knowing the evidence; the mufti is not a pure muqallid but an intermediary.
تغليظ (taghliz): Using harsh or intimidating language in a fatwa to deter wrongdoing, done interpretively based on textual support.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when may a mufti use harsh language (taghliz) in his fatwa?
A) Whenever he feels the questioner deserves it.
B) Only when it is interpretive (mutawwal) and no harm results from its utterance.
C) Only if the ruling is about capital punishment.
D) Never, because it is forbidden in the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must be a mujtahid within the madhhab and may use taghliz only interpretively and without causing harm.

Qada — Judiciary
